<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en"><i>Romeo and Juliet</i></h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet_(disambiguation)" title="Romeo and Juliet (disambiguation)">Romeo and Juliet (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">

<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>

<p><i><b>Romeo and Juliet</b></i> is a <a href="/wiki/Shakespearean_tragedy" title="Shakespearean tragedy">tragedy written by William Shakespeare</a> early in his career about two young <a href="/wiki/Star-crossed" title="Star-crossed">star-crossed</a> lovers whose deaths ultimately reconcile their feuding families. It was among <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">Shakespeare's</a> most popular plays during his lifetime and, along with <i><a href="/wiki/Hamlet" title="Hamlet">Hamlet</a></i>, is one of his most frequently performed plays. Today, the title characters are regarded as <a href="/wiki/Archetype" title="Archetype">archetypal</a> young lovers.</p>
<p><i>Romeo and Juliet</i> belongs to a tradition of tragic <a href="/wiki/Romance_(love)" title="Romance (love)">romances</a> stretching back to antiquity. The plot is based on an Italian tale translated into verse as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tragical_History_of_Romeus_and_Juliet" title="The Tragical History of Romeus and Juliet">The Tragical History of Romeus and Juliet</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Brooke_(poet)" title="Arthur Brooke (poet)">Arthur Brooke</a> in 1562 and retold in prose in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palace_of_Pleasure" title="Palace of Pleasure">Palace of Pleasure</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/William_Painter_(author)" title="William Painter (author)">William Painter</a> in 1567. Shakespeare borrowed heavily from both but expanded the plot by developing a number of supporting characters, particularly <a href="/wiki/Mercutio" title="Mercutio">Mercutio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Count_Paris" title="Count Paris">Paris</a>. Believed to have been written between 1591 and 1595, the play was first published in a <a href="/wiki/Quarto" title="Quarto">quarto</a> version in 1597. The text of the first quarto version was of poor quality, however, and later editions corrected the text to conform more closely with Shakespeare's original.</p>
<p>Shakespeare's use of his poetic <a href="/wiki/Dramatic_structure" title="Dramatic structure">dramatic structure</a> (especially effects such as switching between comedy and tragedy to heighten tension, his expansion of minor characters, and his use of sub-plots to embellish the story) has been praised as an early sign of his dramatic skill. The play ascribes different poetic forms to different characters, sometimes changing the form as the character develops. Romeo, for example, grows more adept at the <a href="/wiki/Sonnet" title="Sonnet">sonnet</a> over the course of the play.</p>
<p><i>Romeo and Juliet</i> has been adapted numerous times for stage, film, musical and opera venues. During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Restoration" title="English Restoration">English Restoration</a>, it was revived and heavily revised by <a href="/wiki/William_Davenant" title="William Davenant">William Davenant</a>. <a href="/wiki/David_Garrick" title="David Garrick">David Garrick</a>'s 18th-century version also modified several scenes, removing material then considered indecent, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georg_Benda" title="Georg Benda">Georg Benda</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Romeo_und_Julie" title="Romeo und Julie">Romeo und Julie</a></i> omitted much of the action and added a happy ending. Performances in the 19th century, including <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_Cushman" title="Charlotte Cushman">Charlotte Cushman</a>'s, restored the original text and focused on greater <a href="/wiki/Realism_(theatre)" title="Realism (theatre)">realism</a>. <a href="/wiki/John_Gielgud" title="John Gielgud">John Gielgud</a>'s 1935 version kept very close to Shakespeare's text and used Elizabethan costumes and staging to enhance the drama. In the 20th and into the 21st century, the play has been adapted in versions as diverse as <a href="/wiki/George_Cukor" title="George Cukor">George Cukor</a>'s 1935 film <i><a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet_(1936_film)" title="Romeo and Juliet (1936 film)">Romeo and Juliet</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Franco_Zeffirelli" title="Franco Zeffirelli">Franco Zeffirelli</a>'s 1968 version <i><a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet_(1968_film)" title="Romeo and Juliet (1968 film)">Romeo and Juliet</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Baz_Luhrmann" title="Baz Luhrmann">Baz Luhrmann</a>'s 1996 MTV-inspired <i><a href="/wiki/Romeo_%2B_Juliet" title="Romeo + Juliet">Romeo + Juliet</a></i>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characters">Characters</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Characters_in_Romeo_and_Juliet" title="Characters in Romeo and Juliet">Characters in Romeo and Juliet</a></div>
<div>
<table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:100%;">
<tr>
<td style="width:50%; text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<dl>
<dt>Ruling house of Verona</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Characters_in_Romeo_and_Juliet#Prince_Escalus" title="Characters in Romeo and Juliet">Prince Escalus</a> is the ruling Prince of <a href="/wiki/Verona" title="Verona">Verona</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Count_Paris" title="Count Paris">Count Paris</a> is a kinsman of Escalus who wishes to marry Juliet.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mercutio" title="Mercutio">Mercutio</a> is another kinsman of Escalus, a friend of Romeo.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt>House of Capulet</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Characters_in_Romeo_and_Juliet#Capulet" title="Characters in Romeo and Juliet">Capulet</a> is the patriarch of the house of Capulet.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Characters_in_Romeo_and_Juliet#Lady_Capulet" title="Characters in Romeo and Juliet">Lady Capulet</a> is the matriarch of the house of Capulet.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Juliet" title="Juliet">Juliet</a> is the 13-year-old daughter of Capulet, the play's female protagonist.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tybalt" title="Tybalt">Tybalt</a> is a cousin of Juliet, the nephew of Lady Capulet.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nurse_(Romeo_and_Juliet)" title="Nurse (Romeo and Juliet)">The Nurse</a> is Juliet's personal attendant and confidante.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rosaline" title="Rosaline">Rosaline</a> is Lord Capulet's niece, Romeo's love in the beginning of the story.</li>
<li>Peter, Sampson, and Gregory are servants of the Capulet household.</li>
</ul>
</td>
<td style="width:50%; text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<dl>
<dt>House of Montague</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Characters_in_Romeo_and_Juliet#Montague" title="Characters in Romeo and Juliet">Montague</a> is the patriarch of the house of Montague.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Characters_in_Romeo_and_Juliet#Montague.27s_wife" title="Characters in Romeo and Juliet">Lady Montague</a> is the matriarch of the house of Montague.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Romeo" title="Romeo">Romeo</a> is the son of Montague, the play's male protagonist.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Benvolio" title="Benvolio">Benvolio</a> is Romeo's cousin and best friend.</li>
<li>Abram and Balthasar are servants of the Montague household.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt>Others</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Friar_Laurence" title="Friar Laurence">Friar Laurence</a> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franciscan" title="Franciscan">Franciscan</a> friar and Romeo's confidant.</li>
<li>Friar John is sent to deliver Friar Laurence's letter to Romeo.</li>
<li>An Apothecary who reluctantly sells Romeo poison.</li>
<li>A Chorus reads a <a href="/wiki/Prologue" title="Prologue">prologue</a> to each of the first two acts.</li>
</ul>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Synopsis">Synopsis</span></h2>
<p>The play, set in <a href="/wiki/Verona" title="Verona">Verona</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, begins with a street brawl between <a href="/wiki/Characters_in_Romeo_and_Juliet#Montague" title="Characters in Romeo and Juliet">Montague</a> and <a href="/wiki/Characters_in_Romeo_and_Juliet#Capulet" title="Characters in Romeo and Juliet">Capulet</a> servants who, like their masters, are sworn enemies. <a href="/wiki/Characters_in_Romeo_and_Juliet#Montague" title="Characters in Romeo and Juliet">Prince Escalus of Verona</a> intervenes and declares that further breach of the peace will be punishable by death. Later, <a href="/wiki/Count_Paris" title="Count Paris">Count Paris</a> talks to Capulet about marrying his daughter <a href="/wiki/Juliet" title="Juliet">Juliet</a>, but Capulet asks Paris to wait another two years and invites him to attend a planned Capulet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ball_(dance)" title="Ball (dance)">ball</a>. Lady Capulet and Juliet's nurse try to persuade Juliet to accept Paris's courtship.</p>
<p>Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/Benvolio" title="Benvolio">Benvolio</a> talks with his cousin <a href="/wiki/Romeo" title="Romeo">Romeo</a>, Montague's son, about Romeo's recent depression. Benvolio discovers that it stems from unrequited infatuation for a girl named <a href="/wiki/Rosaline" title="Rosaline">Rosaline</a>, one of Capulet's nieces. Persuaded by Benvolio and <a href="/wiki/Mercutio" title="Mercutio">Mercutio</a>, Romeo attends the ball at the Capulet house in hopes of meeting Rosaline. However, Romeo instead meets and falls in love with Juliet. Juliet's cousin, <a href="/wiki/Tybalt" title="Tybalt">Tybalt</a>, is enraged at Romeo for sneaking into the ball but is only stopped from killing Romeo by Juliet's father, who doesn't wish to shed blood in his house. After the ball, in what is now called the "balcony scene", Romeo sneaks into the Capulet orchard and overhears Juliet at her window vowing her love to him in spite of her family's hatred of the Montagues. Romeo makes himself known to her and they agree to be married. With the help of <a href="/wiki/Friar_Laurence" title="Friar Laurence">Friar Laurence</a>, who hopes to reconcile the two families through their children's union, they are secretly married the next day.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Francesco_Hayez_053.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="556" height="317" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Francesco_Hayez_053.jpg/220px-Francesco_Hayez_053.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Francesco_Hayez_053.jpg/330px-Francesco_Hayez_053.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Francesco_Hayez_053.jpg/440px-Francesco_Hayez_053.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Francesco_Hayez_053.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>L’ultimo bacio dato a Giulietta da Romeo</i> by <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Hayez" title="Francesco Hayez">Francesco Hayez</a>. Oil on canvas, 1823.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Tybalt, meanwhile, still incensed that Romeo had sneaked into the Capulet ball, challenges him to a duel. Romeo, now considering Tybalt his kinsman, refuses to fight. Mercutio is offended by Tybalt's insolence, as well as Romeo's "vile submission",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> and accepts the duel on Romeo's behalf. Mercutio is fatally wounded when Romeo attempts to break up the fight. Grief-stricken and wracked with guilt, Romeo confronts and slays Tybalt.</p>
<p>Montague argues that Romeo has justly executed Tybalt for the murder of Mercutio. The Prince, now having lost a kinsman in the warring families' feud, exiles Romeo from Verona, under penalty of death if he ever returns. Romeo secretly spends the night in Juliet's chamber, where they <a href="/wiki/Consummation" title="Consummation">consummate</a> their marriage. Capulet, misinterpreting Juliet's grief, agrees to marry her to Count Paris and threatens to disown her when she refuses to become Paris's "joyful bride".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> When she then pleads for the marriage to be delayed, her mother rejects her.</p>
<p>Juliet visits Friar Laurence for help, and he offers her a potion that will put her into a deathlike coma for "two and forty hours".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The Friar promises to send a messenger to inform Romeo of the plan so that he can rejoin her when she awakens. On the night before the wedding, she takes the drug and, when discovered apparently dead, she is laid in the family crypt.</p>
<p>The messenger, however, does not reach Romeo and, instead, Romeo learns of Juliet's apparent death from his servant Balthasar. Heartbroken, Romeo buys poison from an <a href="/wiki/Characters_in_Romeo_and_Juliet#Apothecary" title="Characters in Romeo and Juliet">apothecary</a> and goes to the Capulet <a href="/wiki/Crypt" title="Crypt">crypt</a>. He encounters Paris who has come to mourn Juliet privately. Believing Romeo to be a vandal, Paris confronts him and, in the ensuing battle, Romeo kills Paris. Still believing Juliet to be dead, he drinks the poison. Juliet then awakens and, finding Romeo dead, stabs herself with his dagger. The feuding families and the Prince meet at the tomb to find all three dead. Friar Laurence recounts the story of the two "star-cross'd lovers". The families are reconciled by their children's deaths and agree to end their violent feud. The play ends with the Prince's elegy for the lovers: "For never was a story of more woe/Than this of Juliet and her Romeo."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arthur_Brooke_Tragicall_His.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="374" height="324" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Arthur_Brooke_Tragicall_His.jpg/220px-Arthur_Brooke_Tragicall_His.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Arthur_Brooke_Tragicall_His.jpg/330px-Arthur_Brooke_Tragicall_His.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5b/Arthur_Brooke_Tragicall_His.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arthur_Brooke_Tragicall_His.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Title page of <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Brooke_(poet)" title="Arthur Brooke (poet)">Arthur Brooke</a>'s poem, <i>Romeus and Juliet</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Romeo and Juliet</i> borrows from a tradition of tragic love stories dating back to antiquity. One of these is <a href="/wiki/Pyramus_and_Thisbe" title="Pyramus and Thisbe">Pyramus and Thisbe</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Metamorphoses" title="Metamorphoses">Metamorphoses</a></i>, which contains parallels to Shakespeare's story: the lovers' parents despise each other, and Pyramus falsely believes his lover Thisbe is dead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Ephesian_Tale" title="Ephesian Tale">Ephesiaca</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Xenophon_of_Ephesus" title="Xenophon of Ephesus">Xenophon of Ephesus</a>, written in the 3rd century, also contains several similarities to the play, including the separation of the lovers, and a potion that induces a deathlike sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the earliest references to the names <i>Montague</i> and <i>Capulet</i> is from <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">Divine Comedy</a></i>, who mentions the Montecchi (<i>Montagues</i>) and the Cappelletti (<i>Capulets</i>) in canto six of <a href="/wiki/Purgatorio" title="Purgatorio">Purgatorio</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="poem">
<p>Come and see, you who are negligent,<br/>
Montagues and Capulets, Monaldi and Filippeschi<br/>
One lot already grieving, the other in fear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</blockquote>
<p>However, the reference is part of a polemic against the moral decay of <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lombardy" title="Lombardy">Lombardy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Peninsula" title="Italian Peninsula">Italian Peninsula</a> as a whole; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dante" title="Dante">Dante</a>, through his characters, chastises <a href="/wiki/King_of_the_Romans" title="King of the Romans">German King</a> <a href="/wiki/Albert_I_of_Germany" title="Albert I of Germany">Albert I</a> for neglecting his responsibilities towards Italy ("you who are negligent"), and successive <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">popes</a> for their encroachment from purely spiritual affairs, thus leading to a climate of incessant bickering and warfare between <a href="/wiki/Guelphs_and_Ghibellines" title="Guelphs and Ghibellines">rival political parties</a> in Lombardy. History records the name of the family <i>Montague</i> as being lent to such a political party in <a href="/wiki/Verona" title="Verona">Verona</a>, but that of the <i>Capulets</i> as from a <a href="/wiki/Cremona" title="Cremona">Cremonese</a> family, both of whom play out their conflict in <a href="/wiki/Lombardy" title="Lombardy">Lombardy</a> as a whole rather than within the confines of <a href="/wiki/Verona" title="Verona">Verona</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Higgins.2C_1998:_585_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Higgins.2C_1998:_585-9">[9]</a></sup> Allied to rival political factions, the parties are grieving ("One lot already grieving") because their endless warfare has led to the destruction of both parties,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Higgins.2C_1998:_585_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Higgins.2C_1998:_585-9">[9]</a></sup> rather than a grief from the loss of their ill-fated offspring as the play sets forth, which appears to be a solely poetic creation within this context.</p>
<p>The earliest known version of the <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> tale akin to Shakespeare's play is the story of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mariotto_and_Gianozza&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mariotto and Gianozza (page does not exist)">Mariotto and Gianozza</a> by <a href="/wiki/Masuccio_Salernitano" title="Masuccio Salernitano">Masuccio Salernitano</a>, in the 33rd novel of his <i>Il Novellino</i> published in 1476.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hosley_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hosley-10">[10]</a></sup> Salernitano sets the story in <a href="/wiki/Siena" title="Siena">Siena</a> and insists its events took place in his own lifetime. His version of the story includes the secret marriage, the colluding friar, the fray where a prominent citizen is killed, Mariotto's exile, Gianozza's forced marriage, the potion plot, and the crucial message that goes astray. In this version, Mariotto is caught and beheaded and Gianozza dies of grief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_form">Modern form</span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luigi_da_Porto" title="Luigi da Porto">Luigi da Porto</a> (1485–1529) adapted the story as <i>Giulietta e Romeo</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ital_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-ital-12">[12]</a></sup> and included it in his <i>Historia novellamente ritrovata di due Nobili Amanti</i>, written in 1524 and published posthumously in 1531 in Venice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore38_44_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moore38_44-14">[14]</a></sup> Da Porto drew on <i>Pyramus and Thisbe</i>, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Boccaccio" title="Giovanni Boccaccio">Boccacio</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decameron" title="Decameron">Decameron</a>, and Salernitano's <i>Mariotto e Ganozza</i>, but it is likely that his story is also autobiographical: present as a soldier at a ball on 26 February 1511 at a residence of the Savorgnan clan in <a href="/wiki/Udine" title="Udine">Udine</a>, following a peace ceremony with the opposite Strumieri, Da Porta fell in love with Lucina, the daughter of the house, but relationships of their mentors prevented advances. The next morning, <a href="/wiki/Friulian_Revolt_of_1511" title="Friulian Revolt of 1511">the Savorgnans led an attack on the city</a>, and many members of the Strumieri were murdered. When years later, half-paralyzed from a battle-wound, he wrote <i>Giulietta e Romeo</i> in <a href="/wiki/Montorso_Vicentino" title="Montorso Vicentino">Montorso Vicentino</a> (from where he could see the "castles" of <a href="/wiki/Verona" title="Verona">Verona</a>), he dedicated the <i>novella</i> to <i>bellisima e leggiadra madonna</i> Lucina Savorgnan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ital_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-ital-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Da Porto presented his tale as historically true and claimed it took place a century earlier than Salernitano had it, in the days Verona was ruled by <a href="/wiki/Bartolomeo_II_della_Scala" title="Bartolomeo II della Scala">Bartolomeo II della Scala</a> (anglicized as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prince_Escalus" title="Prince Escalus">Prince Escalus</a>).</p>
<p>Da Porto gave Romeo and Juliet most of its modern form, including the names of the lovers, the rival families of Montecchi and Capuleti, and the location in Verona.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hosley_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hosley-10">[10]</a></sup> He named the friar <a href="/wiki/Friar_Laurence" title="Friar Laurence">Laurence</a> (<i>frate Lorenzo</i>) and introduced the characters <a href="/wiki/Mercutio" title="Mercutio">Mercutio</a> (<i>Marcuccio Guertio</i>), <a href="/wiki/Tybalt" title="Tybalt">Tybalt</a> (<i>Tebaldo Cappelleti</i>), <a href="/wiki/Count_Paris" title="Count Paris">Count Paris</a> (<i>conti (Paride) di <a class="extiw" href="https://it.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lodron" title="it:Lodron">Lodrone</a></i>), the faithful servant, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nurse_(Romeo_and_Juliet_character)" title="Nurse (Romeo and Juliet character)">Giulietta's nurse</a>. Da Porto originated the remaining basic elements of the story: the feuding families, Romeo -left by his mistress- meeting Giulietta at a dance at her house, the love scenes (including the balcony scene), the periods of despair, Romeo killing Giulietta's cousin (Tebaldo), and the families' reconciliation after the lovers' suicides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scarci_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scarci-16">[16]</a></sup> In da Porto's version, Romeo takes poison and Giulietta stabs herself with his dagger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1554, <a href="/wiki/Matteo_Bandello" title="Matteo Bandello">Matteo Bandello</a> published the second volume of his <i>Novelle</i>, which included his version of <i>Giuletta e Romeo</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore38_44_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moore38_44-14">[14]</a></sup> probably written between 1531 and 1545. Bandello lengthened and weighed down the plot while leaving the storyline basically unchanged (though he did introduce <a href="/wiki/Benvolio" title="Benvolio">Benvolio</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scarci_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Scarci-16">[16]</a></sup> Bandello's story was translated into French by <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Boaistuau" title="Pierre Boaistuau">Pierre Boaistuau</a> in 1559 in the first volume of his <i>Histories Tragiques</i>. Boaistuau adds much moralising and sentiment, and the characters indulge in rhetorical outbursts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his 1562 <a href="/wiki/Narrative_poetry" title="Narrative poetry">narrative poem</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tragical_History_of_Romeus_and_Juliet" title="The Tragical History of Romeus and Juliet">The Tragical History of Romeus and Juliet</a></i>, Arthur Brooke translated Boaistuau faithfully but adjusted it to reflect parts of Chaucer's <i><a href="/wiki/Troilus_and_Criseyde" title="Troilus and Criseyde">Troilus and Criseyde</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbons_37_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbons_37-19">[19]</a></sup> There was a trend among writers and playwrights to publish works based on Italian <i>novelles</i>—Italian tales were very popular among theatre-goers—and Shakespeare may well have been familiar with <a href="/wiki/William_Painter_(author)" title="William Painter (author)">William Painter</a>'s 1567 collection of Italian tales titled <i>Palace of Pleasure</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> This collection included a version in prose of the <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> story named <i>"The goodly History of the true and constant love of Romeo and Juliett"</i>. Shakespeare took advantage of this popularity: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Merchant_of_Venice" title="The Merchant of Venice">The Merchant of Venice</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Much_Ado_About_Nothing" title="Much Ado About Nothing">Much Ado About Nothing</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/All%27s_Well_That_Ends_Well" title="All's Well That Ends Well">All's Well That Ends Well</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Measure_for_Measure" title="Measure for Measure">Measure for Measure</a></i>, and <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> are all from Italian <i>novelle</i>. <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> is a dramatisation of Brooke's translation, and Shakespeare follows the poem closely but adds extra detail to both major and minor characters (in particular the Nurse and Mercutio).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christopher_Marlowe" title="Christopher Marlowe">Christopher Marlowe</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Hero_and_Leander_(poem)" title="Hero and Leander (poem)">Hero and Leander</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dido,_Queen_of_Carthage_(play)" title="Dido, Queen of Carthage (play)">Dido, Queen of Carthage</a></i>, both similar stories written in Shakespeare's day, are thought to be less of a direct influence, although they may have helped create an atmosphere in which tragic love stories could thrive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbons_37_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbons_37-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Date_and_text">Date and text</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Romeoandjuliet1597.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="975" data-file-width="588" height="365" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Romeoandjuliet1597.jpg/220px-Romeoandjuliet1597.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Romeoandjuliet1597.jpg/330px-Romeoandjuliet1597.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Romeoandjuliet1597.jpg/440px-Romeoandjuliet1597.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Romeoandjuliet1597.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Title page of the first edition</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>It is unknown when exactly Shakespeare wrote <i>Romeo and Juliet</i>. Juliet's nurse refers to an earthquake she says occurred 11 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> This may refer to the <a href="/wiki/1580_Dover_Straits_earthquake" title="1580 Dover Straits earthquake">Dover Straits earthquake of 1580</a>, which would date that particular line to 1591. Other earthquakes—both in England and in Verona—have been proposed in support of the different dates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arden_Date_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arden_Date-25">[25]</a></sup> But the play's stylistic similarities with <i><a href="/wiki/A_Midsummer_Night%27s_Dream" title="A Midsummer Night's Dream">A Midsummer Night's Dream</a></i> and other plays conventionally dated around 1594–95, place its composition sometime between 1591 and 1595.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> One conjecture is that Shakespeare may have begun a draft in 1591, which he completed in 1595.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shakespeare's <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> was published in two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quarto_(text)" title="Quarto (text)">quarto</a> editions prior to the publication of the <a href="/wiki/First_Folio" title="First Folio">First Folio</a> of 1623. These are referred to as Q1 and Q2. The first printed edition, Q1, appeared in early 1597, printed by John Danter. Because its text contains numerous differences from the later editions, it is labelled a so-called '<a href="/wiki/Bad_quarto" title="Bad quarto">bad quarto</a>'; the 20th-century editor T. J. B. Spencer described it as "a detestable text, probably a reconstruction of the play from the imperfect memories of one or two of the actors", suggesting that it had been pirated for publication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spencer_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spencer-28">[28]</a></sup> An alternative explanation for Q1's shortcomings is that the play (like many others of the time) may have been heavily edited before performance by the playing company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halio_History_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halio_History-29">[29]</a></sup> However, 'the theory, formulated by [Alfred] Pollard," that the 'bad quarto' was "reconstructed from memory by some of the actors is now under attack. Alternative theories are that some or all of 'the bad quartos' are early versions by Shakespeare or abbreviations made either for Shakespeare's company or for other companies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wells_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wells-30">[30]</a></sup> In any event, its appearance in early 1597 makes 1596 the latest possible date for the play's composition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arden_Date_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Arden_Date-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>The superior Q2 called the play <i>The Most Excellent and Lamentable Tragedie of Romeo and Juliet</i>. It was printed in 1599 by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Creede" title="Thomas Creede">Thomas Creede</a> and published by <a href="/wiki/Cuthbert_Burby" title="Cuthbert Burby">Cuthbert Burby</a>. Q2 is about 800 lines longer than Q1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halio_History_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Halio_History-29">[29]</a></sup> Its title page describes it as "Newly corrected, augmented and amended". Scholars believe that Q2 was based on Shakespeare's pre-performance draft (called his <a href="/wiki/Foul_papers" title="Foul papers">foul papers</a>), since there are textual oddities such as variable tags for characters and "false starts" for speeches that were presumably struck through by the author but erroneously preserved by the typesetter. It is a much more complete and reliable text and was reprinted in 1609 (Q3), 1622 (Q4) and 1637 (Q5).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spencer_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Spencer-28">[28]</a></sup> In effect, all later Quartos and Folios of <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> are based on Q2, as are all modern editions since editors believe that any deviations from Q2 in the later editions (whether good or bad) are likely to arise from editors or compositors, not from Shakespeare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halio_History_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Halio_History-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_Folio_Title_Page_of_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1099" data-file-width="1536" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/First_Folio_Title_Page_of_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg/220px-First_Folio_Title_Page_of_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/First_Folio_Title_Page_of_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg/330px-First_Folio_Title_Page_of_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/First_Folio_Title_Page_of_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg/440px-First_Folio_Title_Page_of_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First_Folio_Title_Page_of_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The title page from the <a href="/wiki/First_Folio" title="First Folio">First Folio</a>, printed in 1623.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The First Folio text of 1623 was based primarily on Q3, with clarifications and corrections possibly coming from a theatrical promptbook or Q1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spencer_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-Spencer-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Other Folio editions of the play were printed in 1632 (F2), 1664 (F3), and 1685 (F4).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Modern versions—that take into account several of the Folios and Quartos—first appeared with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicholas_Rowe_(dramatist)" title="Nicholas Rowe (dramatist)">Nicholas Rowe</a>'s 1709 edition, followed by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Pope" title="Alexander Pope">Alexander Pope</a>'s 1723 version. Pope began a tradition of editing the play to add information such as stage directions missing in Q2 by locating them in Q1. This tradition continued late into the <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantic</a> period. Fully annotated editions first appeared in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victorian_period" title="Victorian period">Victorian period</a> and continue to be produced today, printing the text of the play with footnotes describing the sources and culture behind the play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Themes_and_motifs">Themes and motifs</span></h2>
<p>Scholars have found it extremely difficult to assign one specific, overarching <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theme_(literature)" title="Theme (literature)">theme</a> to the play. Proposals for a main theme include a discovery by the characters that human beings are neither wholly good nor wholly evil, but instead are more or less alike,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowling_208_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowling_208-34">[34]</a></sup> awaking out of a dream and into reality, the danger of hasty action, or the power of tragic fate. None of these have widespread support. However, even if an overall theme cannot be found it is clear that the play is full of several small, thematic elements that intertwine in complex ways. Several of those most often debated by scholars are discussed below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Love">Love</span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:23em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<div class="poem">
<p>"<b>Romeo</b><br/>
If I profane with my unworthiest hand<br/>
This holy shrine, the gentle sin is this:<br/>
My lips, two blushing pilgrims, ready stand<br/>
To smooth that rough touch with a tender kiss.<br/>
<b>Juliet</b><br/>
Good pilgrim, you do wrong your hand too much,<br/>
Which mannerly devotion shows in this;<br/>
For saints have hands that pilgrims' hands do touch,<br/>
And palm to palm is holy palmers' kiss."</p>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—<i>Romeo and Juliet</i>, Act I, Scene V<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p><i>Romeo and Juliet</i> is sometimes considered to have no unifying theme, save that of young love.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowling_208_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bowling_208-34">[34]</a></sup> Romeo and Juliet have become emblematic of young lovers and doomed love. Since it is such an obvious subject of the play, several scholars have explored the language and historical context behind the romance of the play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Honegger_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Honegger-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>On their first meeting, Romeo and Juliet use a form of communication recommended by many etiquette authors in Shakespeare's day: metaphor. By using metaphors of saints and sins, Romeo was able to test Juliet's feelings for him in a non-threatening way. This method was recommended by <a href="/wiki/Baldassare_Castiglione" title="Baldassare Castiglione">Baldassare Castiglione</a> (whose works had been translated into English by this time). He pointed out that if a man used a metaphor as an invitation, the woman could pretend she did not understand him, and he could retreat without losing honour. Juliet, however, participates in the metaphor and expands on it. The religious metaphors of "shrine", "pilgrim" and "saint" were fashionable in the poetry of the time and more likely to be understood as romantic rather than blasphemous, as the concept of sainthood was associated with the Catholicism of an earlier age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Later in the play, Shakespeare removes the more daring allusions to Christ's resurrection in the tomb he found in his source work: Brooke's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tragical_History_of_Romeus_and_Juliet" title="The Tragical History of Romeus and Juliet">Romeus and Juliet</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Act_II_Scene_ii_%E2%80%93_Juliet_on_the_Balcony.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1494" data-file-width="1095" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Act_II_Scene_ii_%E2%80%93_Juliet_on_the_Balcony.jpg/220px-Act_II_Scene_ii_%E2%80%93_Juliet_on_the_Balcony.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Act_II_Scene_ii_%E2%80%93_Juliet_on_the_Balcony.jpg/330px-Act_II_Scene_ii_%E2%80%93_Juliet_on_the_Balcony.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Act_II_Scene_ii_%E2%80%93_Juliet_on_the_Balcony.jpg/440px-Act_II_Scene_ii_%E2%80%93_Juliet_on_the_Balcony.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Act_II_Scene_ii_%E2%80%93_Juliet_on_the_Balcony.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Watercolor by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=John_Massey_Wright&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="John Massey Wright (page does not exist)">John Massey Wright</a> of Act II, Scene ii (the balcony scene).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the later balcony scene, Shakespeare has Romeo overhear Juliet's soliloquy, but in Brooke's version of the story, her declaration is done alone. By bringing Romeo into the scene to eavesdrop, Shakespeare breaks from the normal sequence of courtship. Usually, a woman was required to be modest and shy to make sure that her suitor was sincere, but breaking this rule serves to speed along the plot. The lovers are able to skip courting and move on to plain talk about their relationship— agreeing to be married after knowing each other for only one night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Honegger_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Honegger-37">[37]</a></sup> In the final suicide scene, there is a contradiction in the message—in the Catholic religion, suicides were often thought to be condemned to hell, whereas people who die to be with their loves under the "<a href="/wiki/Courtly_love" title="Courtly love">Religion of Love</a>" are joined with their loves in paradise. Romeo and Juliet's love seems to be expressing the "Religion of Love" view rather than the Catholic view. Another point is that although their love is passionate, it is only consummated in marriage, which keeps them from losing the audience's sympathy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>The play arguably equates love and sex with death. Throughout the story, both Romeo and Juliet, along with the other characters, fantasise about <a href="/wiki/Death_(personification)" title="Death (personification)">it as a dark being</a>, often equating it with a lover. Capulet, for example, when he first discovers Juliet's (faked) death, describes it as having <a href="/wiki/Virginity" title="Virginity">deflowered</a> his daughter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Juliet later erotically compares Romeo and death. Right before her suicide she grabs Romeo's dagger, saying "O happy dagger! This is thy sheath. There rust, and let me die."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fate_and_chance">Fate and chance</span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:23em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"O, I am fortune's fool!"</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—Romeo, Act III Scene I<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Scholars are divided on the role of fate in the play. No consensus exists on whether the characters are truly fated to die together or whether the events take place by a series of unlucky chances. Arguments in favour of fate often refer to the description of the lovers as "<a href="/wiki/Star-crossed" title="Star-crossed">star-cross'd</a>". This phrase seems to hint that the stars have predetermined the lovers' future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_W._Draper" title="John W. Draper">John W. Draper</a> points out the parallels between the Elizabethan belief in <a href="/wiki/Humorism" title="Humorism">the four humours</a> and the main characters of the play (for example, Tybalt as a choleric). Interpreting the text in the light of humours reduces the amount of plot attributed to chance by modern audiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Draper_16_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Draper_16-46">[46]</a></sup> Still, other scholars see the play as a series of unlucky chances—many to such a degree that they do not see it as a tragedy at all, but an emotional <a href="/wiki/Melodrama" title="Melodrama">melodrama</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Draper_16_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Draper_16-46">[46]</a></sup> Ruth Nevo believes the high degree to which chance is stressed in the narrative makes <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> a "lesser tragedy" of happenstance, not of character. For example, Romeo's challenging Tybalt is not impulsive; it is, after Mercutio's death, the expected action to take. In this scene, Nevo reads Romeo as being aware of the dangers of flouting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norm_(sociology)" title="Norm (sociology)">social norms</a>, identity, and commitments. He makes the choice to kill, not because of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tragic_flaw" title="Tragic flaw">tragic flaw</a>, but because of circumstance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nevo_241_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nevo_241-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Duality_.28light_and_dark.29">Duality (light and dark)</span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:23em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<div class="poem">
<p>"O brawling love, O loving hate,<br/>
O any thing of nothing first create!<br/>
O heavy lightness, serious vanity,<br/>
Misshapen chaos of well-seeming forms,<br/>
Feather of lead, bright smoke, cold fire, sick health,<br/>
Still-waking sleep, that is not what it is!"</p>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—Romeo, Act I Scene I<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Scholars have long noted Shakespeare's widespread use of light and dark <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imagery_(literature)" title="Imagery (literature)">imagery</a> throughout the play. <a href="/wiki/Caroline_Spurgeon" title="Caroline Spurgeon">Caroline Spurgeon</a> considers the theme of light as "symbolic of the natural beauty of young love" and later critics have expanded on this interpretation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nevo_241_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nevo_241-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parker_663_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parker_663-49">[49]</a></sup> For example, both Romeo and Juliet see the other as light in a surrounding darkness. Romeo describes Juliet as being like the sun,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> brighter than a torch,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> a jewel sparkling in the night,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> and a bright angel among dark clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Even when she lies apparently dead in the tomb, he says her "beauty makes This vault a feasting presence full of light."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Juliet describes Romeo as "day in night" and "Whiter than snow upon a raven's back."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> This contrast of light and dark can be expanded as symbols—contrasting love and hate, youth and age in a metaphoric way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nevo_241_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nevo_241-47">[47]</a></sup> Sometimes these intertwining metaphors create <a href="/wiki/Irony" title="Irony">dramatic irony</a>. For example, Romeo and Juliet's love is a light in the midst of the darkness of the hate around them, but all of their activity together is done in night and darkness, while all of the feuding is done in broad daylight. This paradox of imagery adds atmosphere to the <a href="/wiki/Ethical_dilemma" title="Ethical dilemma">moral dilemma</a> facing the two lovers: loyalty to family or loyalty to love. At the end of the story, when the morning is gloomy and the sun hiding its face for sorrow, light and dark have returned to their proper places, the outward darkness reflecting the true, inner darkness of the family feud out of sorrow for the lovers. All characters now recognise their folly in light of recent events, and things return to the natural order, thanks to the love and death of Romeo and Juliet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parker_663_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Parker_663-49">[49]</a></sup> The "light" theme in the play is also heavily connected to the theme of time since light was a convenient way for Shakespeare to express the passage of time through descriptions of the sun, moon, and stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tanselle_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tanselle-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Time">Time</span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:23em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"These times of woe afford no time to woo."</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—Paris, Act III Scene IV<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Time plays an important role in the language and plot of the play. Both Romeo and Juliet struggle to maintain an imaginary world void of time in the face of the harsh realities that surround them. For instance, when Romeo swears his love to Juliet by the moon, she protests "O swear not by the moon, th'inconstant moon, / That monthly changes in her circled orb, / Lest that thy love prove likewise variable."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> From the very beginning, the lovers are designated as "star-cross'd"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RJ_Prologue_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-RJ_Prologue-60">[60]</a></sup> referring to an <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrologic</a> belief associated with time. Stars were thought to control the fates of humanity, and as time passed, stars would move along their course in the sky, also charting the course of human lives below. Romeo speaks of a foreboding he feels in the stars' movements early in the play, and when he learns of Juliet's death, he defies the stars' course for him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Draper_16_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-Draper_16-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another central theme is haste: Shakespeare's <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> spans a period of four to six days, in contrast to Brooke's poem's spanning nine months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tanselle_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tanselle-57">[57]</a></sup> Scholars such as G. Thomas Tanselle believe that time was "especially important to Shakespeare" in this play, as he used references to "short-time" for the young lovers as opposed to references to "long-time" for the "older generation" to highlight "a headlong rush towards doom".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tanselle_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tanselle-57">[57]</a></sup> Romeo and Juliet fight time to make their love last forever. In the end, the only way they seem to defeat time is through a death that makes them immortal through art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lucking_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lucking-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Time is also connected to the theme of light and dark. In Shakespeare's day, plays were most often performed at noon or in the afternoon in broad daylight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> This forced the playwright to use words to create the illusion of day and night in his plays. Shakespeare uses references to the night and day, the stars, the moon, and the sun to create this illusion. He also has characters frequently refer to days of the week and specific hours to help the audience understand that time has passed in the story. All in all, no fewer than 103 references to time are found in the play, adding to the illusion of its passage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_and_interpretation">Criticism and interpretation</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_history">Critical history</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Samuel_Pepys.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2343" data-file-width="2000" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Samuel_Pepys.jpg/220px-Samuel_Pepys.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Samuel_Pepys.jpg/330px-Samuel_Pepys.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Samuel_Pepys.jpg/440px-Samuel_Pepys.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Samuel_Pepys.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portrait of the earliest recorded critic of the play, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Pepys" title="Samuel Pepys">Samuel Pepys</a>, by <a href="/wiki/John_Hayls" title="John Hayls">John Hayls</a>. Oil on canvas, 1666.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The earliest known critic of the play was diarist <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Pepys" title="Samuel Pepys">Samuel Pepys</a>, who wrote in 1662: "it is a play of itself the worst that I ever heard in my life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShakeCrit415_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShakeCrit415-65">[65]</a></sup> Poet <a href="/wiki/John_Dryden" title="John Dryden">John Dryden</a> wrote 10 years later in praise of the play and its comic character Mercutio: "Shakespear show'd the best of his skill in his <i>Mercutio</i>, and he said himself, that he was forc'd to kill him in the third Act, to prevent being killed by him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShakeCrit415_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-ShakeCrit415-65">[65]</a></sup> Criticism of the play in the 18th century was less sparse, but no less divided. Publisher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicholas_Rowe_(dramatist)" title="Nicholas Rowe (dramatist)">Nicholas Rowe</a> was the first critic to ponder the theme of the play, which he saw as the just punishment of the two feuding families. In mid-century, writer <a href="/wiki/Charles_Gildon" title="Charles Gildon">Charles Gildon</a> and philosopher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Kames" title="Lord Kames">Lord Kames</a> argued that the play was a failure in that it did not follow the classical rules of drama: the tragedy must occur because of some <a href="/wiki/Hamartia" title="Hamartia">character flaw</a>, not an accident of fate. Writer and critic <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Samuel Johnson</a>, however, considered it one of Shakespeare's "most pleasing" plays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShakeCrit410_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShakeCrit410-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the later part of the 18th and through the 19th century, criticism centred on debates over the moral message of the play. Actor and playwright <a href="/wiki/David_Garrick" title="David Garrick">David Garrick</a>'s 1748 adaptation excluded Rosaline: Romeo abandoning her for Juliet was seen as fickle and reckless. Critics such as <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dibdin" title="Charles Dibdin">Charles Dibdin</a> argued that Rosaline had been purposely included in the play to show how reckless the hero was, and that this was the reason for his tragic end. Others argued that Friar Laurence might be Shakespeare's spokesman in his warnings against undue haste. With the advent of the 20th century, these moral arguments were disputed by critics such as <a href="/wiki/Richard_Green_Moulton" title="Richard Green Moulton">Richard Green Moulton</a>: he argued that accident, and not some character flaw, led to the lovers' deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dramatic_structure">Dramatic structure</span></h3>
<p>In <i>Romeo and Juliet</i>, Shakespeare employs several dramatic techniques that have garnered praise from critics; most notably the abrupt shifts from comedy to tragedy (an example is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punning" title="Punning">punning</a> exchange between Benvolio and Mercutio just before Tybalt arrives). Before Mercutio's death in Act three, the play is largely a comedy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> After his accidental demise, the play suddenly becomes serious and takes on a tragic tone. When Romeo is banished, rather than executed, and Friar Laurence offers Juliet a plan to reunite her with Romeo, the audience can still hope that all will end well. They are in a "breathless state of suspense" by the opening of the last scene in the tomb: If Romeo is delayed long enough for the Friar to arrive, he and Juliet may yet be saved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> These shifts from hope to despair, reprieve, and new hope, serve to emphasise the tragedy when the final hope fails and both the lovers die at the end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halio_20_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halio_20-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shakespeare also uses sub-plots to offer a clearer view of the actions of the main characters. For example, when the play begins, Romeo is in love with Rosaline, who has refused all of his advances. Romeo's infatuation with her stands in obvious contrast to his later love for Juliet. This provides a comparison through which the audience can see the seriousness of Romeo and Juliet's love and marriage. Paris' love for Juliet also sets up a contrast between Juliet's feelings for him and her feelings for Romeo. The formal language she uses around Paris, as well as the way she talks about him to her Nurse, show that her feelings clearly lie with Romeo. Beyond this, the <a href="/wiki/Subplot" title="Subplot">sub-plot</a> of the Montague–Capulet feud overarches the whole play, providing an atmosphere of hate that is the main contributor to the play's tragic end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halio_20_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Halio_20-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span></h3>
<p>Shakespeare uses a variety of poetic forms throughout the play. He begins with a 14-line <a href="/wiki/Prologue" title="Prologue">prologue</a> in the form of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakespearean_sonnet" title="Shakespearean sonnet">Shakespearean sonnet</a>, spoken by a Chorus. Most of <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> is, however, written in <a href="/wiki/Blank_verse" title="Blank verse">blank verse</a>, and much of it in strict <a href="/wiki/Iambic_pentameter" title="Iambic pentameter">iambic pentameter</a>, with less rhythmic variation than in most of Shakespeare's later plays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halio_1998:_51_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halio_1998:_51-71">[71]</a></sup> In choosing forms, Shakespeare matches the poetry to the character who uses it. Friar Laurence, for example, uses <a href="/wiki/Sermon" title="Sermon">sermon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sententiae" title="Sententiae">sententiae</a> forms, and the Nurse uses a unique <a href="/wiki/Blank_verse" title="Blank verse">blank verse</a> form that closely matches <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colloquial_speech" title="Colloquial speech">colloquial speech</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halio_1998:_51_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Halio_1998:_51-71">[71]</a></sup> Each of these forms is also moulded and matched to the emotion of the scene the character occupies. For example, when Romeo talks about Rosaline earlier in the play, he attempts to use the <a href="/wiki/Petrarchan_sonnet" title="Petrarchan sonnet">Petrarchan sonnet</a> form. Petrarchan sonnets were often used by men to exaggerate the beauty of women who were impossible for them to attain, as in Romeo's situation with Rosaline. This sonnet form is used by Lady Capulet to describe Count Paris to Juliet as a handsome man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> When Romeo and Juliet meet, the poetic form changes from the Petrarchan (which was becoming archaic in Shakespeare's day) to a then more contemporary sonnet form, using "pilgrims" and "saints" as metaphors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Finally, when the two meet on the balcony, Romeo attempts to use the sonnet form to pledge his love, but Juliet breaks it by saying "Dost thou love me?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> By doing this, she searches for true expression, rather than a poetic exaggeration of their love.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Juliet uses monosyllabic words with Romeo, but uses formal language with Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Other forms in the play include an <a href="/wiki/Epithalamium" title="Epithalamium">epithalamium</a> by Juliet, a <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">rhapsody</a> in Mercutio's <a href="/wiki/Queen_Mab" title="Queen Mab">Queen Mab</a> speech, and an <a href="/wiki/Elegy" title="Elegy">elegy</a> by Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Shakespeare saves his prose style most often for the common people in the play, though at times he uses it for other characters, such as Mercutio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Humour, also, is important: scholar Molly Mahood identifies at least 175 puns and wordplays in the text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Many of these jokes are sexual in nature, especially those involving Mercutio and the Nurse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychoanalytic_criticism">Psychoanalytic criticism</span></h3>
<p>Early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychoanalytic_criticism" title="Psychoanalytic criticism">psychoanalytic critics</a> saw the problem of <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> in terms of Romeo's impulsiveness, deriving from "ill-controlled, partially disguised aggression", which leads both to Mercutio's death and to the double suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> is not considered to be exceedingly psychologically complex, and sympathetic psychoanalytic readings of the play make the tragic male experience equivalent with sicknesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Norman Holland, writing in 1966, considers Romeo's dream<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> as a realistic "wish fulfilling fantasy both in terms of Romeo's adult world and his hypothetical childhood at stages oral, phallic and oedipal" – while acknowledging that a dramatic character is not a human being with mental processes separate from those of the author.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Critics such as <a href="/wiki/Julia_Kristeva" title="Julia Kristeva">Julia Kristeva</a> focus on the hatred between the families, arguing that this hatred is the cause of Romeo and Juliet's passion for each other. That hatred manifests itself directly in the lovers' language: Juliet, for example, speaks of "my only love sprung from my only hate"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> and often expresses her passion through an anticipation of Romeo's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> This leads on to speculation as to the playwright's psychology, in particular to a consideration of Shakespeare's grief for the death of his son, <a href="/wiki/Hamnet_Shakespeare" title="Hamnet Shakespeare">Hamnet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feminist_criticism">Feminist criticism</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Feminist_literary_criticism" title="Feminist literary criticism">Feminist literary critics</a> argue that the blame for the family feud lies in Verona's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarchal_society" title="Patriarchal society">patriarchal society</a>. For Coppélia Kahn, for example, the strict, masculine code of violence imposed on Romeo is the main force driving the tragedy to its end. When Tybalt kills Mercutio, Romeo shifts into this violent mode, regretting that Juliet has made him so "effeminate".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> In this view, the younger males "become men" by engaging in violence on behalf of their fathers, or in the case of the servants, their masters. The feud is also linked to male virility, as the numerous jokes about maidenheads aptly demonstrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Juliet also submits to a female code of docility by allowing others, such as the Friar, to solve her problems for her. Other critics, such as Dympna Callaghan, look at the play's feminism from a <a href="/wiki/Historicism" title="Historicism">historicist</a> angle, stressing that when the play was written the feudal order was being challenged by increasingly centralised government and the advent of capitalism. At the same time, emerging <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puritan" title="Puritan">Puritan</a> ideas about marriage were less concerned with the "evils of female sexuality" than those of earlier eras, and more sympathetic towards love-matches: when Juliet dodges her father's attempt to force her to marry a man she has no feeling for, she is challenging the patriarchal order in a way that would not have been possible at an earlier time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Queer_theory">Queer theory</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Drury_Lane_Playbill_of_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="955" height="354" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Drury_Lane_Playbill_of_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg/220px-Drury_Lane_Playbill_of_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Drury_Lane_Playbill_of_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg/330px-Drury_Lane_Playbill_of_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Drury_Lane_Playbill_of_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg/440px-Drury_Lane_Playbill_of_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Drury_Lane_Playbill_of_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The playbill from a 1753 production at the <a href="/wiki/Theatre_Royal,_Drury_Lane" title="Theatre Royal, Drury Lane">Theatre Royal</a> in Drury Lane starring <a href="/wiki/David_Garrick" title="David Garrick">David Garrick</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A number of critics have found the character of Mercutio to have unacknowledged homoerotic desire for Romeo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Jonathan Goldberg examined the sexuality of Mercutio and Romeo utilising "<a href="/wiki/Queer_theory" title="Queer theory">queer theory</a>" in <i>Queering the Renaissance</i>, comparing their friendship with sexual love. Mercutio, in friendly conversation, mentions Romeo's <a href="/wiki/Phallus" title="Phallus">phallus</a>, suggesting traces of <a href="/wiki/Homoeroticism" title="Homoeroticism">homoeroticism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> An example is his joking wish "To raise a spirit in his mistress' circle ... letting it there stand / Till she had laid it and conjured it down."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Romeo's homoeroticism can also be found in his attitude to Rosaline, a woman who is distant and unavailable and brings no hope of offspring. As Benvolio argues, she is best replaced by someone who will reciprocate. Shakespeare's <a href="/wiki/Procreation_sonnets" title="Procreation sonnets">procreation sonnets</a> describe another young man who, like Romeo, is having trouble creating offspring and who may be seen as being a homosexual. Goldberg believes that Shakespeare may have used Rosaline as a way to express homosexual problems of procreation in an acceptable way. In this view, when Juliet says "...that which we call a rose, by any other name would smell as sweet",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> she may be raising the question of whether there is any difference between the beauty of a man and the beauty of a woman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Balcony_Scene">The Balcony Scene</span></h3>
<p>The balcony scene was introduced by Da Porto in 1524. He had Romeo walk frequently by her house, "sometimes climbing to her chamber window" and wrote "It happened one night, as love ordained, when the moon shone unusually bright, that whilst Romeo was climbing the balcony, the young lady ... opened the window, and looking out saw him".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> After this they have a conversation in which they declare eternal love to each other. A few decades later, Bandello greatly expanded this scene, diverging from the familiar one: Julia has her nurse deliver a letter asking Romeo to come to her window with a rope ladder, and he climbs the balcony with the help of his servant, Julia and the nurse (the servants discreetly withdraw after this).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scarci_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Scarci-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nevertheless, in October 2014, Lois Leveen speculated in <a href="/wiki/The_Atlantic" title="The Atlantic">The Atlantic</a> that the original Shakespeare play did not contain a balcony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheAtlantic_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-TheAtlantic-98">[98]</a></sup> The word, <i>balcone</i>, did not exist in the English language until two years after Shakespeare's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> The balcony was certainly used in <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Otway" title="Thomas Otway">Thomas Otway</a>'s 1679 play, <i>The History and Fall of Caius Marius</i>, which had borrowed much of its story from <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> and placed the two lovers in a balcony reciting a speech similar to that between Romeo and Juliet. Leveen suggested that during the 18th century, <a href="/wiki/David_Garrick" title="David Garrick">David Garrick</a> chose to use a balcony in his adaptation and revival of <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> and modern adaptations have continued this tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheAtlantic_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-TheAtlantic-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shakespeare.27s_day">Shakespeare's day</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_Richard_Burbage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="891" data-file-width="700" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Young_Richard_Burbage.jpg/220px-Young_Richard_Burbage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Young_Richard_Burbage.jpg/330px-Young_Richard_Burbage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Young_Richard_Burbage.jpg/440px-Young_Richard_Burbage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_Richard_Burbage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Richard_Burbage" title="Richard Burbage">Richard Burbage</a>, probably the first actor to portray <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romeo_Montague" title="Romeo Montague">Romeo</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halio_97_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halio_97-100">[100]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Romeo and Juliet</i> ranks with <i><a href="/wiki/Hamlet" title="Hamlet">Hamlet</a></i> as one of Shakespeare's most performed plays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-awesome_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-awesome-101">[101]</a></sup> Its many adaptations have made it one of his most enduring and famous stories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-awesome_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-awesome-101">[101]</a></sup> Even in Shakespeare's lifetime it was extremely popular. Scholar Gary Taylor measures it as the sixth most popular of Shakespeare's plays, in the period after the death of <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Marlowe" title="Christopher Marlowe">Christopher Marlowe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Kyd" title="Thomas Kyd">Thomas Kyd</a> but before the ascendancy of <a href="/wiki/Ben_Jonson" title="Ben Jonson">Ben Jonson</a> during which Shakespeare was London's dominant playwright.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> The date of the first performance is unknown. The First Quarto, printed in 1597, says that "it hath been often (and with great applause) plaid publiquely", setting the first performance before that date. The <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chamberlain%27s_Men" title="Lord Chamberlain's Men">Lord Chamberlain's Men</a> were certainly the first to perform it. Besides their strong connections with Shakespeare, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Quarto" title="Second Quarto">Second Quarto</a> actually names one of its actors, <a href="/wiki/William_Kempe" title="William Kempe">Will Kemp</a>, instead of Peter in a line in Act five. <a href="/wiki/Richard_Burbage" title="Richard Burbage">Richard Burbage</a> was probably the first Romeo, being the company's actor, and Master Robert Goffe (a boy) the first Juliet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halio_97_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Halio_97-100">[100]</a></sup> The premiere is likely to have been at "<a href="/wiki/The_Theatre" title="The Theatre">The Theatre</a>", with other early productions at "<a href="/wiki/Curtain_Theatre" title="Curtain Theatre">The Curtain</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> is one of the first Shakespearean plays to have been performed outside England: a shortened and simplified version was performed in <a href="/wiki/N%C3%B6rdlingen" title="Nördlingen">Nördlingen</a> in 1604.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Restoration_and_18th-century_theatre">Restoration and 18th-century theatre</span></h3>
<p>All theatres were closed down by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puritan" title="Puritan">puritan</a> government on 6 September 1642. Upon the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Restoration" title="English Restoration">restoration</a> of the monarchy in 1660, two patent companies (the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Company" title="King's Company">King's Company</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Duke%27s_Company" title="Duke's Company">Duke's Company</a>) were established, and the existing theatrical repertoire divided between them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mary_Saunderson_17th_century.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="605" data-file-width="480" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Mary_Saunderson_17th_century.jpg/220px-Mary_Saunderson_17th_century.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Mary_Saunderson_17th_century.jpg/330px-Mary_Saunderson_17th_century.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Mary_Saunderson_17th_century.jpg/440px-Mary_Saunderson_17th_century.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mary_Saunderson_17th_century.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Mary_Saunderson" title="Mary Saunderson">Mary Saunderson</a>, probably the first woman to play Juliet professionally</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sir <a href="/wiki/William_Davenant" title="William Davenant">William Davenant</a> of the Duke's Company staged a 1662 adaptation in which <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_Harris_(actor)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Henry Harris (actor) (page does not exist)">Henry Harris</a> played Romeo, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Betterton" title="Thomas Betterton">Thomas Betterton</a> Mercutio, and Betterton's wife <a href="/wiki/Mary_Saunderson" title="Mary Saunderson">Mary Saunderson</a> Juliet: she was probably the first woman to play the role professionally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halio_100_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halio_100-106">[106]</a></sup> Another version closely followed Davenant's adaptation and was also regularly performed by the Duke's Company. This was a tragicomedy by James Howard, in which the two lovers survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Otway" title="Thomas Otway">Thomas Otway</a>'s <i>The History and Fall of Caius Marius</i>, one of the more extreme of the Restoration adaptations of Shakespeare, debuted in 1680. The scene is shifted from Renaissance Verona to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Rome</a>; Romeo is Marius, Juliet is Lavinia, the feud is between patricians and plebeians; Juliet/Lavinia wakes from her potion before Romeo/Marius dies. Otway's version was a hit, and was acted for the next seventy years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halio_100_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Halio_100-106">[106]</a></sup> His innovation in the closing scene was even more enduring, and was used in adaptations throughout the next 200 years: <a href="/wiki/Theophilus_Cibber" title="Theophilus Cibber">Theophilus Cibber</a>'s adaptation of 1744, and <a href="/wiki/David_Garrick" title="David Garrick">David Garrick</a>'s of 1748 both used variations on it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> These versions also eliminated elements deemed inappropriate at the time.
