For example, Garrick's version transferred all language describing Rosaline to Juliet, to heighten the idea of faithfulness and downplay the love-at-first-sight theme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> In 1750 a "Battle of the Romeos" began, with <a href="/wiki/Spranger_Barry" title="Spranger Barry">Spranger Barry</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Susannah_Maria_Arne" title="Susannah Maria Arne">Susannah Maria Arne</a> (Mrs. Theophilus Cibber) at <a href="/wiki/Royal_Opera_House" title="Royal Opera House">Covent Garden</a> versus <a href="/wiki/David_Garrick" title="David Garrick">David Garrick</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Anne_Bellamy" title="George Anne Bellamy">George Anne Bellamy</a> at <a href="/wiki/Theatre_Royal,_Drury_Lane" title="Theatre Royal, Drury Lane">Drury Lane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>The earliest known production in North America was an amateur one: on 23 March 1730, a physician named Joachimus Bertrand placed an advertisement in the <i>Gazette</i> newspaper in New York, promoting a production in which he would play the apothecary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The first professional performances of the play in North America were those of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Company" title="American Company">Hallam Company</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th-century_theatre">19th-century theatre</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Harvard_Theatre_Collection_-_Charlotte_and_Susan_Cushman_TCS_45.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1395" data-file-width="1013" height="303" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Harvard_Theatre_Collection_-_Charlotte_and_Susan_Cushman_TCS_45.jpg/220px-Harvard_Theatre_Collection_-_Charlotte_and_Susan_Cushman_TCS_45.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Harvard_Theatre_Collection_-_Charlotte_and_Susan_Cushman_TCS_45.jpg/330px-Harvard_Theatre_Collection_-_Charlotte_and_Susan_Cushman_TCS_45.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Harvard_Theatre_Collection_-_Charlotte_and_Susan_Cushman_TCS_45.jpg/440px-Harvard_Theatre_Collection_-_Charlotte_and_Susan_Cushman_TCS_45.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Harvard_Theatre_Collection_-_Charlotte_and_Susan_Cushman_TCS_45.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The American Cushman sisters, <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_Cushman" title="Charlotte Cushman">Charlotte</a> and <a href="/wiki/Susan_Webb_Cushman" title="Susan Webb Cushman">Susan</a>, as Romeo and Juliet in 1846</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Garrick's altered version of the play was very popular, and ran for nearly a century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halio_100_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-Halio_100-106">[106]</a></sup> Not until 1845 did Shakespeare's original return to the stage in the United States with the sisters <a href="/wiki/Susan_Webb_Cushman" title="Susan Webb Cushman">Susan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_Cushman" title="Charlotte Cushman">Charlotte Cushman</a> as Juliet and Romeo, respectively,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> and then in 1847 in Britain with <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Phelps" title="Samuel Phelps">Samuel Phelps</a> at <a href="/wiki/Sadler%27s_Wells_Theatre" title="Sadler's Wells Theatre">Sadler's Wells Theatre</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Cushman adhered to Shakespeare's version, beginning a string of eighty-four performances. Her portrayal of Romeo was considered genius by many. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> wrote: "For a long time Romeo has been a convention. Miss Cushman's Romeo is a creative, a living, breathing, animated, ardent human being."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victoria_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Victoria of the United Kingdom">Queen Victoria</a> wrote in her journal that "no-one would ever have imagined she was a woman".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Cushman's success broke the Garrick tradition and paved the way for later performances to return to the original storyline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halio_100_106-3"><a href="#cite_note-Halio_100-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>Professional performances of Shakespeare in the mid-19th century had two particular features: firstly, they were generally <a href="/wiki/Star_vehicle" title="Star vehicle">star vehicles</a>, with supporting roles cut or marginalised to give greater prominence to the central characters. Secondly, they were "pictorial", placing the action on spectacular and elaborate sets (requiring lengthy pauses for scene changes) and with the frequent use of <a href="/wiki/Tableau_vivant" title="Tableau vivant">tableaux</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Irving" title="Henry Irving">Henry Irving</a>'s 1882 production at the <a href="/wiki/Lyceum_Theatre,_London" title="Lyceum Theatre, London">Lyceum Theatre</a> (with himself as Romeo and <a href="/wiki/Ellen_Terry" title="Ellen Terry">Ellen Terry</a> as Juliet) is considered an archetype of the pictorial style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> In 1895, Sir <a href="/wiki/Johnston_Forbes-Robertson" title="Johnston Forbes-Robertson">Johnston Forbes-Robertson</a> took over from Irving, and laid the groundwork for a more natural portrayal of Shakespeare that remains popular today. Forbes-Robertson avoided the showiness of Irving and instead portrayed a down-to-earth Romeo, expressing the poetic dialogue as realistic prose and avoiding melodramatic flourish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>American actors began to rival their British counterparts. <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Booth" title="Edwin Booth">Edwin Booth</a> (brother to <a href="/wiki/John_Wilkes_Booth" title="John Wilkes Booth">John Wilkes Booth</a>) and Mary McVicker (soon to be Edwin's wife) opened as Romeo and Juliet at the sumptuous <a href="/wiki/Booth%27s_Theatre" title="Booth's Theatre">Booth's Theatre</a> (with its European-style <a href="/wiki/Stage_machinery" title="Stage machinery">stage machinery</a>, and an air conditioning system unique in New York) on 3 February 1869. Some reports said it was one of the most elaborate productions of <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> ever seen in America; it was certainly the most popular, running for over six weeks and earning over $60,000<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> (equal to about $1,080,000 today).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-US_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-121">[121]</a></sup> The programme noted that: "The tragedy will be produced in strict accordance with historical propriety, in every respect, following closely the text of Shakespeare."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first professional performance of the play in Japan may have been George Crichton Miln's company's production, which toured to <a href="/wiki/Yokohama" title="Yokohama">Yokohama</a> in 1890.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Throughout the 19th century, <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> had been Shakespeare's most popular play, measured by the number of professional performances. In the 20th century it would become the second most popular, behind <i><a href="/wiki/Hamlet" title="Hamlet">Hamlet</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th-century_theatre">20th-century theatre</span></h3>
<p>In 1933, the play was revived by actress <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Cornell" title="Katharine Cornell">Katharine Cornell</a> and her director husband <a href="/wiki/Guthrie_McClintic" title="Guthrie McClintic">Guthrie McClintic</a> and was taken on a seven-month nationwide tour throughout the United States. It starred <a href="/wiki/Orson_Welles" title="Orson Welles">Orson Welles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brian_Aherne" title="Brian Aherne">Brian Aherne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Basil_Rathbone" title="Basil Rathbone">Basil Rathbone</a>. The production was a modest success, and so upon the return to New York, Cornell and McClintic revised it and for the first time, the play was presented with almost all the scenes intact, including the Prologue. The new production opened in December 1934 with <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Richardson" title="Ralph Richardson">Ralph Richardson</a> as Mercutio and <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Evans_(actor)" title="Maurice Evans (actor)">Maurice Evans</a> as Romeo. Critics wrote that Cornell was "the finest Juliet of her time", "endlessly haunting", and "the most lovely and enchanting Juliet our present-day theatre has seen".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_John_Gielgud_2_by_Carl_Van_Vechten_cropped.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="406" data-file-width="316" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Portrait_of_John_Gielgud_2_by_Carl_Van_Vechten_cropped.jpeg/220px-Portrait_of_John_Gielgud_2_by_Carl_Van_Vechten_cropped.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d5/Portrait_of_John_Gielgud_2_by_Carl_Van_Vechten_cropped.jpeg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_John_Gielgud_2_by_Carl_Van_Vechten_cropped.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/John_Gielgud" title="John Gielgud">John Gielgud</a>, who was among the more famous 20th-century actors to play Romeo, Friar Laurence and Mercutio on stage</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Gielgud" title="John Gielgud">John Gielgud</a>'s <a href="/wiki/No%C3%ABl_Coward_Theatre" title="Noël Coward Theatre">New Theatre</a> production in 1935 featured Gielgud and <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Olivier" title="Laurence Olivier">Laurence Olivier</a> as Romeo and Mercutio, exchanging roles six weeks into the run, with <a href="/wiki/Peggy_Ashcroft" title="Peggy Ashcroft">Peggy Ashcroft</a> as Juliet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Gielgud used a scholarly combination of Q1 and Q2 texts, and organised the set and costumes to match as closely as possible to the <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_era" title="Elizabethan era">Elizabethan period</a>. His efforts were a huge success at the box office, and set the stage for increased <a href="/wiki/Historical_realism" title="Historical realism">historical realism</a> in later productions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Olivier later compared his performance and Gielgud's: "John, all spiritual, all spirituality, all beauty, all abstract things; and myself as all earth, blood, humanity ... I've always felt that John missed the lower half and that made me go for the other ... But whatever it was, when I was playing Romeo I was carrying a torch, I was trying to sell realism in Shakespeare."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Peter_Brook" title="Peter Brook">Peter Brook</a>'s 1947 version was the beginning of a different style of <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> performances. Brook was less concerned with realism, and more concerned with translating the play into a form that could communicate with the modern world. He argued, "A production is only correct at the moment of its correctness, and only good at the moment of its success."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Brook excluded the final reconciliation of the families from his performance text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levenson_2000:_87_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levenson_2000:_87-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout the century, audiences, influenced by the cinema, became less willing to accept actors distinctly older than the teenage characters they were playing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> A significant example of more youthful casting was in <a href="/wiki/Franco_Zeffirelli" title="Franco Zeffirelli">Franco Zeffirelli</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Vic" title="Old Vic">Old Vic</a> production in 1960, with <a href="/wiki/John_Stride" title="John Stride">John Stride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Judi_Dench" title="Judi Dench">Judi Dench</a>, which would serve as the basis for his <a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet_(1968_film)" title="Romeo and Juliet (1968 film)">1968 film</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levenson_2000:_87_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-Levenson_2000:_87-130">[130]</a></sup> Zeffirelli borrowed from Brook's ideas, altogether removing around a third of the play's text to make it more accessible. In an interview with <i>The Times</i>, he stated that the play's "twin themes of love and the total breakdown of understanding between two generations" had contemporary relevance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Recent performances often set the play in the contemporary world. For example, in 1986 the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Shakespeare_Company" title="Royal Shakespeare Company">Royal Shakespeare Company</a> set the play in modern <a href="/wiki/Verona" title="Verona">Verona</a>. Switchblades replaced swords, feasts and balls became drug-laden rock parties, and Romeo committed suicide by <a href="/wiki/Hypodermic_needle" title="Hypodermic needle">hypodermic needle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> In 1997, the <a href="/wiki/Folger_Shakespeare_Library#Activities" title="Folger Shakespeare Library">Folger Shakespeare Theatre</a> produced a version set in a typical suburban world. Romeo sneaks into the Capulet barbecue to meet Juliet, and Juliet discovers Tybalt's death while in class at school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>The play is sometimes given a historical setting, enabling audiences to reflect on the underlying conflicts. For example, adaptations have been set in the midst of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Israeli-Palestinian_conflict" title="Israeli-Palestinian conflict">Israeli-Palestinian conflict</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a> era in South Africa,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> and in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Pueblo_Revolt" title="Pueblo Revolt">Pueblo Revolt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Ustinov" title="Peter Ustinov">Peter Ustinov</a>'s 1956 comic adaptation, <i><a href="/wiki/Romanoff_and_Juliet_(play)" title="Romanoff and Juliet (play)">Romanoff and Juliet</a></i>, is set in a fictional mid-European country in the depths of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howard_2000:_297_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howard_2000:_297-138">[138]</a></sup> A mock-Victorian revisionist version of <i>Romeo and Juliet</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> final scene (with a happy ending, Romeo, Juliet, Mercutio and Paris restored to life, and Benvolio revealing that he is Paris's love, Benvolia, in disguise) forms part of the 1980 stage-play <i><a href="/wiki/The_Life_and_Adventures_of_Nicholas_Nickleby_(play)" title="The Life and Adventures of Nicholas Nickleby (play)">The Life and Adventures of Nicholas Nickleby</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> <i>Shakespeare’s R&amp;J</i>, by Joe Calarco, spins the classic in a modern tale of gay teenage awakening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> A recent comedic musical adaptation was <a href="/wiki/The_Second_City" title="The Second City">The Second City</a>'s <i>The Second City's Romeo and Juliet Musical: The People vs. Friar Laurence, the Man Who Killed Romeo and Juliet</i>, set in modern times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 19th and 20th century, <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> has often been the choice of Shakespeare plays to open a classical theatre company, beginning with <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Booth" title="Edwin Booth">Edwin Booth</a>'s inaugural production of that play in his theatre in 1869, the newly reformed company of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Vic" title="Old Vic">Old Vic</a> in 1929 with <a href="/wiki/John_Gielgud" title="John Gielgud">John Gielgud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martita_Hunt" title="Martita Hunt">Martita Hunt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Webster" title="Margaret Webster">Margaret Webster</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Riverside_Shakespeare_Company" title="Riverside Shakespeare Company">Riverside Shakespeare Company</a> in its founding production in New York City in 1977, which used the 1968 film of <a href="/wiki/Franco_Zeffirelli" title="Franco Zeffirelli">Franco Zeffirelli</a>'s production as its inspiration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2013, <i><a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet_(2013_Broadway_play)" title="Romeo and Juliet (2013 Broadway play)">Romeo and Juliet</a></i> ran on Broadway at <a href="/wiki/Richard_Rodgers_Theatre" title="Richard Rodgers Theatre">Richard Rodgers Theatre</a> from 19 September to 8 December for 93 regular performances after 27 previews starting on 24 August with <a href="/wiki/Orlando_Bloom" title="Orlando Bloom">Orlando Bloom</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Condola_Rashad" title="Condola Rashad">Condola Rashad</a> in the starring roles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ballet">Ballet</span></h4>
<p>The best-known ballet version is <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Prokofiev" title="Sergei Prokofiev">Prokofiev</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet_(Prokofiev)" title="Romeo and Juliet (Prokofiev)">Romeo and Juliet</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Originally commissioned by the <a href="/wiki/Mariinsky_Ballet" title="Mariinsky Ballet">Kirov Ballet</a>, it was rejected by them when Prokofiev attempted a happy ending, and was rejected again for the experimental nature of its music. It has subsequently attained an "immense" reputation, and has been choreographed by <a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet_(Cranko)" title="Romeo and Juliet (Cranko)">John Cranko</a> (1962) and <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_MacMillan" title="Kenneth MacMillan">Kenneth MacMillan</a> (1965) among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1977, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Smuin" title="Michael Smuin">Michael Smuin</a>'s production of one of the play's most dramatic and impassioned dance interpretations was debuted in its entirety by <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Ballet" title="San Francisco Ballet">San Francisco Ballet</a>. This production was the first full-length ballet to be broadcast by the <a href="/wiki/PBS" title="PBS">PBS</a> series "<a href="/wiki/Great_Performances" title="Great Performances">Great Performances</a>: Dance in America"; it aired in 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:23em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<div class="poem">
<p>"Romeo loved Juliet<br/>
Juliet, she felt the same<br/>
When he put his arms around her<br/>
He said Julie, baby, you're my flame<br/>
Thou givest fever ..."</p>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—<a href="/wiki/Peggy_Lee" title="Peggy Lee">Peggy Lee</a>'s rendition of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fever_(1956_song)" title="Fever (1956 song)">Fever</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>At least 27 operas have been based on Romeo and Juliet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> The earliest, <i><a href="/wiki/Romeo_und_Julie" title="Romeo und Julie">Romeo und Julie</a></i> in 1776, a <a href="/wiki/Singspiel" title="Singspiel">Singspiel</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georg_Benda" title="Georg Benda">Georg Benda</a>, omits much of the action of the play and most of its characters, and has a happy ending. It is occasionally revived. The best-known is <a href="/wiki/Charles_Gounod" title="Charles Gounod">Gounod</a>'s 1867 <i><a href="/wiki/Rom%C3%A9o_et_Juliette" title="Roméo et Juliette">Roméo et Juliette</a></i> (libretto by <a href="/wiki/Jules_Barbier" title="Jules Barbier">Jules Barbier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michel_Carr%C3%A9" title="Michel Carré">Michel Carré</a>), a critical triumph when first performed and frequently revived today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Bellini" title="Vincenzo Bellini">Bellini's</a> <i><a href="/wiki/I_Capuleti_e_i_Montecchi" title="I Capuleti e i Montecchi">I Capuleti e i Montecchi</a></i> is also revived from time to time, but has sometimes been judged unfavourably because of its perceived liberties with Shakespeare; however, Bellini and his librettist, <a href="/wiki/Felice_Romani" title="Felice Romani">Felice Romani</a>, worked from Italian sources—principally Romani's libretto for <i><a href="/wiki/Giulietta_e_Romeo_(Vaccai)" title="Giulietta e Romeo (Vaccai)">Giulietta e Romeo</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Nicola_Vaccai" title="Nicola Vaccai">Nicola Vaccai</a>—rather than directly adapting Shakespeare's play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Among later operas there is <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Sutermeister" title="Heinrich Sutermeister">Heinrich Sutermeister</a>'s 1940 work <i><a href="/wiki/Romeo_und_Julia" title="Romeo und Julia">Romeo und Julia</a></i>.</p>
<p><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rom%C3%A9o_et_Juliette_(symphony)" title="Roméo et Juliette (symphony)">Roméo et Juliette</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Hector_Berlioz" title="Hector Berlioz">Berlioz</a> is a "symphonie dramatique", a large-scale work in three parts for mixed voices, chorus, and orchestra, which premiered in 1839.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pyotr_Ilyich_Tchaikovsky" title="Pyotr Ilyich Tchaikovsky">Tchaikovsky</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet_(Tchaikovsky)" title="Romeo and Juliet (Tchaikovsky)"><i>Romeo and Juliet</i> Fantasy-Overture</a> (1869, revised 1870 and 1880) is a 15-minute <a href="/wiki/Symphonic_poem" title="Symphonic poem">symphonic poem</a>, containing the famous melody known as the "love theme".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Tchaikovsky's device of repeating the same musical theme at the ball, in the balcony scene, in Juliet's bedroom and in the tomb<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> has been used by subsequent directors: for example <a href="/wiki/Nino_Rota" title="Nino Rota">Nino Rota</a>'s love theme is used in a similar way in the 1968 film of the play, as is <a href="/wiki/Des%27ree" title="Des'ree">Des'ree</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Kissing_You_(Des%27ree_song)" title="Kissing You (Des'ree song)">Kissing You</a> in the 1996 film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Other classical composers influenced by the play include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_Hugh_Pearson" title="Henry Hugh Pearson">Henry Hugh Pearson</a> (<i>Romeo and Juliet, overture for orchestra</i>, Op. 86), <a href="/wiki/Johan_Svendsen" title="Johan Svendsen">Svendsen</a> (<i>Romeo og Julie</i>, 1876), <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Delius" title="Frederick Delius">Delius</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/A_Village_Romeo_and_Juliet" title="A Village Romeo and Juliet">A Village Romeo and Juliet</a></i>, 1899–1901), <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Stenhammar" title="Wilhelm Stenhammar">Stenhammar</a> (<i>Romeo och Julia</i>, 1922), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dmitri_Kabalevsky" title="Dmitri Kabalevsky">Kabalevsky</a> (<i>Incidental Music to Romeo and Juliet</i>, Op. 56, 1956).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>The play influenced several <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a> works, including <a href="/wiki/Peggy_Lee" title="Peggy Lee">Peggy Lee</a>'s "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fever_(1956_song)" title="Fever (1956 song)">Fever</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Duke_Ellington" title="Duke Ellington">Duke Ellington</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Such_Sweet_Thunder" title="Such Sweet Thunder">Such Sweet Thunder</a></i> contains a piece entitled "The Star-Crossed Lovers"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> in which the pair are represented by tenor and alto saxophones: critics noted that Juliet's sax dominates the piece, rather than offering an image of equality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> The play has frequently influenced <a href="/wiki/Popular_music" title="Popular music">popular music</a>, including works by <a href="/wiki/The_Supremes" title="The Supremes">The Supremes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Springsteen" title="Bruce Springsteen">Bruce Springsteen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tom_Waits" title="Tom Waits">Tom Waits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lou_Reed" title="Lou Reed">Lou Reed</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Taylor_Swift" title="Taylor Swift">Taylor Swift</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> The most famous such track is <a href="/wiki/Dire_Straits" title="Dire Straits">Dire Straits</a>' "<a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet_(Dire_Straits_song)" title="Romeo and Juliet (Dire Straits song)">Romeo and Juliet</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most famous musical theatre adaptation is <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Side_Story_(musical)" title="West Side Story (musical)">West Side Story</a></i> with music by <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Bernstein" title="Leonard Bernstein">Leonard Bernstein</a> and lyrics by <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Sondheim" title="Stephen Sondheim">Stephen Sondheim</a>. It débuted on Broadway in 1957 and in the West End in 1958 and became a popular film in 1961. This version updated the setting to mid-20th-century New York City and the warring families to ethnic gangs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> Other musical adaptations include <a href="/wiki/Terrence_Mann" title="Terrence Mann">Terrence Mann</a>'s 1999 rock musical <i>William Shakespeare's Romeo and Juliet</i>, co-written with Jerome Korman,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> Gérard Presgurvic's 2001 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rom%C3%A9o_et_Juliette,_de_la_Haine_%C3%A0_l%27Amour" title="Roméo et Juliette, de la Haine à l'Amour">Roméo et Juliette, de la Haine à l'Amour</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Riccardo_Cocciante" title="Riccardo Cocciante">Riccardo Cocciante</a>'s 2007 <i><a href="/wiki/Giulietta_e_Romeo_(musical)" title="Giulietta e Romeo (musical)">Giulietta &amp; Romeo</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_and_art">Literature and art</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Johann_Heinrich_F%C3%BCssli_060.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2584" data-file-width="2024" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Johann_Heinrich_F%C3%BCssli_060.jpg/170px-Johann_Heinrich_F%C3%BCssli_060.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Johann_Heinrich_F%C3%BCssli_060.jpg/255px-Johann_Heinrich_F%C3%BCssli_060.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Johann_Heinrich_F%C3%BCssli_060.jpg/340px-Johann_Heinrich_F%C3%BCssli_060.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Johann_Heinrich_F%C3%BCssli_060.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Romeo at Juliet's Deathbed</i>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fuseli" title="Henry Fuseli">Henry Fuseli</a>, 1809</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Romeo and Juliet</i> had a profound influence on subsequent literature. Before then, romance had not even been viewed as a worthy topic for tragedy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> In Harold Bloom's words, Shakespeare "invented the formula that the sexual becomes the erotic when crossed by the shadow of death".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Of Shakespeare's works, <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> has generated the most—and the most varied—adaptations, including prose and verse narratives, drama, opera, orchestral and choral music, ballet, film, television and painting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> The word "Romeo" has even become synonymous with "male lover" in English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Romeo and Juliet</i> was parodied in Shakespeare's own lifetime: <a href="/wiki/Henry_Porter_(playwright)" title="Henry Porter (playwright)">Henry Porter</a>'s <i>Two Angry Women of Abingdon</i> (1598) and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Dekker_(writer)" title="Thomas Dekker (writer)">Thomas Dekker</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Blurt,_Master_Constable" title="Blurt, Master Constable">Blurt, Master Constable</a></i> (1607) both contain balcony scenes in which a virginal heroine engages in bawdy wordplay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> The play directly influenced later <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literary works</a>. For example, the preparations for a performance form a major plot arc in <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Nickleby" title="Nicholas Nickleby">Nicholas Nickleby</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Romeo and Juliet</i> is one of Shakespeare's most-illustrated works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> The first known illustration was a woodcut of the tomb scene,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> thought to be by Elisha Kirkall, which appeared in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicholas_Rowe_(dramatist)" title="Nicholas Rowe (dramatist)">Nicholas Rowe</a>'s 1709 edition of Shakespeare's plays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Five paintings of the play were commissioned for the <a href="/wiki/Boydell_Shakespeare_Gallery" title="Boydell Shakespeare Gallery">Boydell Shakespeare Gallery</a> in the late 18th century, one representing each of the five acts of the play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> The 19th-century fashion for "pictorial" performances led to directors drawing on paintings for their inspiration, which in turn influenced painters to depict actors and scenes from the theatre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> In the 20th century, the play's most iconic visual images have derived from its popular film versions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2014, Simon &amp; Schuster will publish <i>Juliet's Nurse</i>, a novel by historian and former college professor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lois_M._Leveen" title="Lois M. Leveen">Lois M. Leveen</a> imagining the fourteen years leading up to the events in the play from the point of view of the nurse. The nurse has the third largest number of lines in the original play; only the eponymous characters have more lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Screen">Screen</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet_on_screen" title="Romeo and Juliet on screen">Romeo and Juliet on screen</a></div>
<p><i>Romeo and Juliet</i> may be the most-filmed play of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brode_2001:_42_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brode_2001:_42-179">[179]</a></sup> The most notable theatrical releases were <a href="/wiki/George_Cukor" title="George Cukor">George Cukor</a>'s multi-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award" title="Academy Award">Oscar</a>-nominated <a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet_(1936_film)" title="Romeo and Juliet (1936 film)">1936 production</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franco_Zeffirelli" title="Franco Zeffirelli">Franco Zeffirelli</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet_(1968_film)" title="Romeo and Juliet (1968 film)">1968 version</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Baz_Luhrmann" title="Baz Luhrmann">Baz Luhrmann</a>'s 1996 MTV-inspired <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare%27s_Romeo_%2B_Juliet" title="William Shakespeare's Romeo + Juliet">Romeo + Juliet</a></i>. The latter two were both, in their time, the highest-grossing Shakespeare film ever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> was first filmed in the silent era, by <a href="/wiki/Georges_M%C3%A9li%C3%A8s" title="Georges Méliès">Georges Méliès</a>, although his film is now lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brode_2001:_42_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brode_2001:_42-179">[179]</a></sup> The play was first heard on film in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hollywood_Revue_of_1929" title="The Hollywood Revue of 1929">The Hollywood Revue of 1929</a></i>, in which <a href="/wiki/John_Gilbert_(actor)" title="John Gilbert (actor)">John Gilbert</a> recited the balcony scene opposite <a href="/wiki/Norma_Shearer" title="Norma Shearer">Norma Shearer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leslie_Howard_and_Norma_Shearer_as_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="1182" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Leslie_Howard_and_Norma_Shearer_as_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg/220px-Leslie_Howard_and_Norma_Shearer_as_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Leslie_Howard_and_Norma_Shearer_as_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg/330px-Leslie_Howard_and_Norma_Shearer_as_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Leslie_Howard_and_Norma_Shearer_as_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg/440px-Leslie_Howard_and_Norma_Shearer_as_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leslie_Howard_and_Norma_Shearer_as_Romeo_and_Juliet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Leslie Howard as Romeo and Norma Shearer as Juliet, in the 1936 MGM film directed by <a href="/wiki/George_Cukor" title="George Cukor">George Cukor</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shearer and <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Howard_(actor)" title="Leslie Howard (actor)">Leslie Howard</a>, with a combined age over 75, played the teenage lovers in <a href="/wiki/George_Cukor" title="George Cukor">George Cukor</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer" title="Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer">MGM</a> <a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet_(1936_film)" title="Romeo and Juliet (1936 film)">1936 film version</a>. Neither critics nor the public responded enthusiastically. Cinemagoers considered the film too "arty", staying away as they had from Warner's <i><a href="/wiki/A_Midsummer_Night%27s_Dream_(1935_film)" title="A Midsummer Night's Dream (1935 film)">A Midsummer Night Dream</a></i> a year before: leading to Hollywood abandoning the Bard for over a decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Renato_Castellani" title="Renato Castellani">Renato Castellani</a> won the <i><a href="/wiki/Golden_Lion#Golden_Lion" title="Golden Lion">Grand Prix</a></i> at the <a href="/wiki/Venice_Film_Festival" title="Venice Film Festival">Venice Film Festival</a> for his <a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet_(1954_film)" title="Romeo and Juliet (1954 film)">1954 film of <i>Romeo and Juliet</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> His Romeo, <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Harvey" title="Laurence Harvey">Laurence Harvey</a>, was already an experienced screen actor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> By contrast, Susan Shentall, as Juliet, was a secretarial student who was discovered by the director in a London pub, and was cast for her "pale sweet skin and honey-blonde hair".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stephen Orgel describes <a href="/wiki/Franco_Zeffirelli" title="Franco Zeffirelli">Franco Zeffirelli</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet_(1968_film)" title="Romeo and Juliet (1968 film)">1968 <i>Romeo and Juliet</i></a> as being "full of beautiful young people, and the camera and the lush technicolour, make the most of their sexual energy and good looks".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> Zeffirelli's teenage leads, <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Whiting" title="Leonard Whiting">Leonard Whiting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Olivia_Hussey" title="Olivia Hussey">Olivia Hussey</a>, had virtually no previous acting experience but performed capably and with great maturity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> Zeffirelli has been particularly praised,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> for his presentation of the duel scene as bravado getting out-of-control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> The film courted controversy by including a nude wedding-night scene<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> while Olivia Hussey was only fifteen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Baz_Luhrmann" title="Baz Luhrmann">Baz Luhrmann</a>'s 1996 <i><a href="/wiki/Romeo_%2B_Juliet" title="Romeo + Juliet">Romeo + Juliet</a></i> and its <a href="/wiki/Romeo_%2B_Juliet_(soundtrack)" title="Romeo + Juliet (soundtrack)">accompanying soundtrack</a> successfully targeted the "<a href="/wiki/MTV_Generation" title="MTV Generation">MTV Generation</a>": a young audience of similar age to the story's characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> Far darker than Zeffirelli's version, the film is set in the "crass, violent and superficial society" of Verona Beach and Sycamore Grove.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_DiCaprio" title="Leonardo DiCaprio">Leonardo DiCaprio</a> was Romeo and <a href="/wiki/Claire_Danes" title="Claire Danes">Claire Danes</a> was Juliet.</p>
<p>The play has been widely adapted for TV and film. In 1960, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Ustinov" title="Peter Ustinov">Peter Ustinov</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cold-war" title="Cold-war">cold-war</a> stage parody, <i><a href="/wiki/Romanoff_and_Juliet_(film)" title="Romanoff and Juliet (film)">Romanoff and Juliet</a></i> was filmed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howard_2000:_297_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-Howard_2000:_297-138">[138]</a></sup> The 1961 film of <i><a href="/wiki/West_Side_Story_(film)" title="West Side Story (film)">West Side Story</a></i>—set among New York gangs–featured the Jets as white youths, equivalent to Shakespeare's Montagues, while the Sharks, equivalent to the Capulets, are Puerto Rican.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> The 1994 film <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_Punk_(1994_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The Punk (1994 film) (page does not exist)">The Punk</a></i> uses both the rough plot outline of <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> and names many of the characters in ways that reflect the characters in the play. In 2006, Disney's <i><a href="/wiki/High_School_Musical" title="High School Musical">High School Musical</a></i> made use of <i>Romeo and Juliet</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> plot, placing the two young lovers in rival high school cliques instead of feuding families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> Film-makers have frequently featured characters performing scenes from <i>Romeo and Juliet</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Conceit" title="Conceit">conceit</a> of dramatising Shakespeare writing <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> has been used several times,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/John_Madden_(director)" title="John Madden (director)">John Madden</a>'s 1998 <i><a href="/wiki/Shakespeare_in_Love" title="Shakespeare in Love">Shakespeare in Love</a></i>, in which Shakespeare writes the play against the backdrop of his own doomed love affair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anime_series" title="Anime series">anime series</a> produced by <a href="/wiki/Gonzo_(company)" title="Gonzo (company)">Gonzo</a> and <a href="/wiki/SKY_Perfect_Well_Think" title="SKY Perfect Well Think">SKY Perfect Well Think</a>, called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romeo_x_Juliet" title="Romeo x Juliet">Romeo x Juliet</a></i>, was made in 2007 and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet_(2013_film)" title="Romeo and Juliet (2013 film)">2013 version</a> is the latest English-language film based on the play. In 2013, <a href="/wiki/Sanjay_Leela_Bhansali" title="Sanjay Leela Bhansali">Sanjay Leela Bhansali</a> directed the Bollywood film <i><a href="/wiki/Goliyon_Ki_Raasleela_Ram-Leela" title="Goliyon Ki Raasleela Ram-Leela">Goliyon Ki Raasleela Ram-Leela</a></i>, a contemporary version of the play which starred <a href="/wiki/Ranveer_Singh" title="Ranveer Singh">Ranveer Singh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deepika_Padukone" title="Deepika Padukone">Deepika Padukone</a> in leading roles. The film was a commercial and critical success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> In February 2014, <a href="/wiki/BroadwayHD" title="BroadwayHD">BroadwayHD</a> released a filmed version of the <a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet_(2013_Broadway_play)" title="Romeo and Juliet (2013 Broadway play)">2013 Broadway Revival</a> of <i>Romeo and Juliet</i>. The production starred <a href="/wiki/Orlando_Bloom" title="Orlando Bloom">Orlando Bloom</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Condola_Rashad" title="Condola Rashad">Condola Rashad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> The film was released internationally in April 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_social_media_and_virtual_world_productions">Modern social media and virtual world productions</span></h3>
<p>In April and May 2010 the Royal Shakespeare Company and the Mudlark Production Company presented a version of the play, entitled <i>Such Tweet Sorrow</i>, as an improvised, real-time series of tweets on Twitter. The production used RSC actors who engaged with the audience as well each other, performing not from a traditional script but a "Grid" developed by the Mudlark production team and writers Tim Wright and Bethan Marlow. The performers also make use of other media sites such as YouTube for pictures and video.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup></p>

</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h3>
<div class="refbegin" style="">
<p>All references to <i>Romeo and Juliet</i>, unless otherwise specified, are taken from the Arden Shakespeare second edition (Gibbons, 1980) based on the Q2 text of 1599, with elements from Q1 of 1597.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> Under its referencing system, which uses Roman numerals, II.ii.33 means act 2, scene 2, line 33, and a 0 in place of a scene number refers to the prologue to the act.</p>

<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">When performed in the central yard of an inn and in public theaters such as the <a href="/wiki/Globe_Theatre" title="Globe Theatre">Globe Theatre</a> the only source of lighting was daylight. When performed at Court, inside the stately home of a member of the nobility and in indoor theaters such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blackfriars_theatre" title="Blackfriars theatre">Blackfriars theatre</a> candle lighting was used and plays could be performed even at night.</span></li>
